What is free public wifi? While many would say "it is wifi that is provided at no monetary cost," does that make it free? // // Join [[Kerry]] on her experience at Starbucks, or follow [[Kim]] and her mother as they encounter [[LinkNYC]] for the first time to learn more about free public wifi. Kerry is a senior in college with finals approaching. She needed a break from the library so she went to Starbucks for a change of scenery during her studies. After a few hours her friend [[Kelly]] comes to joinOn a beautiful spring day Kim and her mom Katie took a trip to New York City. They walked through [[Central Park]], ate a pretzel on a street corner and window shopped on Fifth Ave. After hours of exploring [[Katie and Kim]] were ready to head to dinner. In New York City free public wifi is offered through “LinkNYC.” LinkNYC stands, as seen in the photo, can be found in 1,768 different places across the five boroughs of New York City and offer a user a variety of options. // <img src= 'According to Panday, Most people assume that all Wi-Fi networks available in public places like airports, hotels and cafes have security built-in. That's not always the case. When consumers log onto an [[unsecure network]], hackers can steal information as it travels across the web, sell it on the dark web for profit or even use the information to drain consumer bank accounts.' CBS Philly did a story on the risks of free wifi in [[cafes]]." <iframe width="560" height="315" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/KchE_SNkZCU" frameborder="0" allow="accelerometer; autoplay; encrypted-media; gyroscope; picture-in-picture" allowfullscreen></iframe> // // [[Continue]] with Kerrys storyKelly: " As you can see Starbucks Wi-Fi is not always what you think. According to Mengyuan when users connect to free public wifi, hackers are able to determine the key strokes via hand patterns used to unlock a phone and input various passwords. The hacker does not have to be nearby which makes the situation [[even more dangerous]] to public users." The second they mentioned the name Mary's face dropped.Mary: "Please tell me you didn't use it. " Katie: "Of course we did. Kim: "My instagram is [[private]], so no one can see my posts if I don't approve it first."Mary: "While that is good to hear, that doesn't mean that the stand is not collecting your personal information. The city does not own the stands, an outside source provides it free to the city, with an underlying cost." According to Green, the LinkNYC kiosks "are owned and operated by Sidewalk Labs - a susidiary of Alphabet, the parent company of Google." "Sidewalk Labs gathers data about everyone who uses the services, enabling it to generate targeted advertising. Connecting to public Wi-Fi network therefore comes at the cost of providing data about your location and behavior [[to private companies]]." (Green, 2)Kelly: "When you connect to a public Wi-Fi network everyone who is connected to that network has access to all of the internet traffic of everyone else on the network." Kerry: "[[What does that mean]]?" Kelly: "That means that if I connect to the Starbucks Wi-Fi, I could see all of the sites that everyone else is visiting. And if the websites aren't secured, I could see someone's username and passwords. //// Kerry: " What? I had no idea. I've been using Starbucks Wi-Fi for years. " //// Kelly: "Yes, most people are unaware of the consequences of free public Wi-Fi, especially in cafes. Luckily, bank websites are usually secured. Katie: "Hey Kim do you have your phone cord we can [[charge]] it for a minute too."The first time a user connects to the wifi at a LinkNYC stand, it will be using the free wifi. After connecting the the free wifi the user will have the ability to access a key for the private wifi. //// The free wifi is an open network similar to those in cafes that rely on the web server for privacy. The private network secures all communication with the Link Kiosk but is only available on certain devices.
